<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9f9c34ebc4e746b09ecd17fffc6159b3">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f088f70c-c8d1-4d8c-80e5-b99adf182634'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Prof. Haoshen Zhou</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d142192350" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A high-entropy strategy is applied to design a new P2 phase cathode material for sodium-ion batteries, aiming to improve stability in deeply desodiated states. This material exhibits superior reversibility with 0.61 Na extracted, attributed to triggered lattice-oxygen redox reaction activity and facilitated Na<sup>+</sup> migration. As a result, it demonstrates excellent initial Coulombic efficiency, high energy density, and lengthened cycling performance.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f943ba1b-7967-4aa8-96f1-87586012113f/anie202405620-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f943ba1b-7967-4aa8-96f1-87586012113f/anie202405620-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/44449f1f-a400-4f02-bfa8-08c2c755e588/anie202405620-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f943ba1b-7967-4aa8-96f1-87586012113f/anie202405620-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d142192353" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Manganese-based layered oxides are currently of significant interest as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries due to their low toxicity and high specific capacity. However, the practical applications are impeded by sluggish intrinsic Na<sup>+</sup> migration and poor structure stability as a result of Jahn–Teller distortion and complicated phase transition. In this study, a high-entropy strategy is proposed to enhance the high-voltage capacity and cycling stability. The designed P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Cu<sub>0.08</sub>Ni<sub>0.09</sub>Fe<sub>0.18</sub>Ti<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> achieves a deeply desodiation and delivers charging capacity of 158.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to 0.61 Na with a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 98.2 %. The charge compensation is attributed to the cationic and anionic redox reactions conjunctively. Moreover, the crystal structure is effectively stabilized, leading to a slight variation of lattice parameters. This research carries implications for the expedited development of low-cost, high-energy-density cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202405620-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202405620-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable batteries gained extensive utilization as energy storage devices in portable electronics, electric vehicles, stationary grids and other fields. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as highly promising complementary selection to current commercial lithium-ion batteries, drawing substantial research interest in recent years by virtue of the same working principles, the inexpensive raw materials and the similar materials design.<sup><b>[1]</b></sup> The performance of SIBs are significantly impacted by the specific capacity and operating voltage of the cathode materials. The layered sodium transition metal (TM) oxides Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (x≤1) have attracted widespread attention due to convenient synthetic method and promising electrochemical properties. The structures of Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> can be classified into P-phase and O-phase types, where P and O represents prismatic and octahedral crystal structures, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0005_R_d142192342e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Manganese-based layered oxides stand out from pack because of the abundant reserves, low toxicity, and affordability of raw materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0008_R_d142192342e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun.">3</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (NMO) exhibits promising specific capacity and charge compensation reversibility, aligning with the evolving needs of SIBs in distributed energy storage system.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0009_R_d142192342e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Rev.">4</a></sup></span> The electrochemical performance of NMO is primarily influenced by the complex phase transition and unstable microstructure evolution. Galvanostatic current cycles induce noticeable changes in lattice parameters due to heightened O−O repulsion.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0010_R_d142192342e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Engl.">5</a></sup></span> Notably, the issue intensifies at voltages exceeding 3.5 V, as high Na<sup>+</sup> extraction triggers a phase transition from P-type to O-type.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0011_R_d142192342e657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> This structure transition can exacerbate desodiation steric hindrance, hindering Na<sup>+</sup> migration and extraction.</p>
         
         <p>The approach of doping is a paradigmatic and rational strategy to manipulate the microscopic mechanism, and reaction kinetics, due to its convenient manipulation and diverse choices.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0014_R_d142192342e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> In some cases, the introduction of electrochemically active metal elements such as Ni, Fe, and Co can enhance of the operating voltage and facilitate the migration of Na, albeit without effectively improving the structural stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0017_R_d142192342e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> On the other hand, doping with inactive elements like Li, Mg, and Zn, have been shown to moderate phase transition issues and enable a significant amount of Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation through the induction of anionic redox reactions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0021_R_d142192342e673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> However, these approaches still face challenges such as poor reversibility, voltage hysteresis, and sluggish kinetics, which need to be addressed.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0025_R_d142192342e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> Recently, high-entropy oxides (HEOs) incorporated multiple elements into a single-phase structure have drawn the attention of investigators.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0028_R_d142192342e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> Through the stabilization of structure, HEOs have shown promising results in improving the cycling performance and rate capability of host materials.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report on a low-cost Mn-based HEO P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Cu<sub>0.08</sub>Ni<sub>0.09</sub>Fe<sub>0.18</sub>Ti<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (MCNFT) as a cathode material for SIBs. The high-entropy strategy is employed to facilitate the intrinsic Na<sup>+</sup> migration and vitalize the high-voltage capacity and cyclability. The detailed charging compensation mechanism was investigated based on the ex situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) results, revealing the involvement of both cationic redox and anionic redox reactions. The presence of Cu<sup>2+</sup> and Ni<sup>2+</sup> activate the oxygen redox reactivity and facilitate desodiation, while Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> moderate the P-type to O-type phase transition and stabilize the crystal structure. In situ XRD measurements were utilized to reveal the crystal structure evolution. Consequently, the MCNFT sample exhibits an impressive charging capacity of 160.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with outstanding reversibility. This promising strategy holds the potential to advance the design and application of low-cost, high-energy-density cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202405620-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202405620-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202405620-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202405620-sec-0003-title"> Crystal Structure</h3>
            
            <p>The MCNFT and NMO samples were synthesized via a convenient solid-state reaction method, and the experimental details can be found in the Supporting Information. To elucidate the crystal structure of both MCNFT and NMO samples, synchronized X-ray diffraction (sXRD) experiments were carried out with Rietveld refinement via GSAS-II, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0001">1a</a> and Figure S1 (Supporting Information), respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0032_R_d142192342e731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">12</a></sup></span> The results show that MCNFT and NMO samples can be indexed to pure P2-type structure with a space group of P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc and the comparison of lattice parameters are summarized in Table S1 (Supporting Information). The refinement results of MCNFT sample are <i>a</i>=<i>b</i>=2.90519(0) Å, <i>c</i>=11.19973(9) Å, and V=81.863(0) Å<sup>3</sup>, which are larger than those of NMO in all dimensions. The stoichiometry of MCNFT and NMO were analyzed by the inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES), and the results, as shown in Table S2 (Supporting Information), demonstrate good consistency with designed system. The layered structure was characterized by the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) and the image exhibited in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0001">1b</a>. The distance between adjacent TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs measures 0.56 nm, in good accordance with the lattice parameters. Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0001">1c</a> displays the energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mappings, revealing a uniform distribution of Na, Mn, Cu, Ni, Fe, Ti, O elements in MCNFT.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202405620-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ec1ffda4-79c0-4972-b835-f1786d2c9979/anie202405620-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ec1ffda4-79c0-4972-b835-f1786d2c9979/anie202405620-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/57de7c2a-03f6-42b3-ba8c-dd630a512581/anie202405620-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ec1ffda4-79c0-4972-b835-f1786d2c9979/anie202405620-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202405620-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202405620" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Structural characterizations of MCNFT. a) Rietveld refinement pattern of sXRD of MCNFT. b) HRTEM image and c) EDS mappings of MCNFT. Scale bar, 200 nm. d) Schematic illustration of the P2 structure via the a axis. e) Comparison of the spacings d<sub>(O-Na-O)</sub> and d<sub>(O-TM-O)</sub> of MCNFT and NMO. f) Calculated projected density of states (pDOS) of MCNFT and NMO.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The schematic illustration of the crystal structure of P2 phase is shown in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0001">1d</a>. The doped ions (Ni<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, and Ti<sup>4+</sup>) are introduced into the TM layers and expected to partially replace Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> ion sites based on the high-entropy strategy. The interlayer spacings between the layers of O-Na-O (d<sub>O-Na-O</sub>) and O-TM-O (d<sub>O-TM-O</sub>) are calculated to be 3.426 Å and 2.175 Å of MCNFT, respectively, as well as 3.384 Å and 2.186 Å of NMO (Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0001">1e</a>). It is determined that this high entropy strategy leads to the enlargement of Na layers and the shrinkage of TMO<sub>2</sub> layers, suggesting the enhanced structure stability and improved Na<sup>+</sup> migration.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0033_R_d142192342e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span> The projected density of states (pDOS) based on DFT calculations was utilized to estimate the activity of lattice oxygen and the schematic illustration of Na<sub>16</sub>Mn<sub>15</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>1</sub>O<sub>48</sub> was shown in Figure S2 (Supporting Information). As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0001">1f</a>, the total DOS structure of MCNFT exhibits metallic behavior while there is a band gap existing in NMO. In the vicinity of Fermi level (E<sub>F</sub>), the electronic density of states attributed to O 2p orbitals in MCNFT sample is higher than that in NMO, indicating that the anionic redox activity of O<sup>2−</sup>/O<sup>−</sup> has been enhanced obviously.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0034_R_d142192342e839" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></sup></span> It can be deduced that the anionic redox reaction would contribute to additional capacity during the initial charging of the MCNFT sample.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202405620-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202405620-sec-0004-title"> Electrochemical Characteristic</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical properties of the designed MCNFT sample were evaluated alongside normal NMO sample versus metallic Na between 2.0 V and 4.5 V at the rate of 0.1 C, as showing in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0002">2</a>. During the first charging process to 4.5 V, there are 0.61 Na<sup>+</sup> of MCNFT and 0.36 Na<sup>+</sup> of NMO extracted from the layer structures, as displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0002">2b</a>. And the initial Coulombic efficiency (defined as the ratio of charging capacity to discharging capacity, ICE) of MCNFT is 98.2 %, which is much higher than the 57.7 % of NMO. This MCNFT achieves almost complete desodiation and a sustained reversible capacity of 161 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> on cycling. After fifteen cycles, the capacity retention increases from 84.5 % of NMO to 97.3 % of MCNFT (Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0002">2c</a>). Clearly, the MCNFT demonstrates significantly improved capability for deep sodium removal and reversibility. Line-scan electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) was used to reveal the chemical evolution of MCNFT collected from sample particle. Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0002">2d</a> compares the O K-edge and Mn L-edge spectra from surface to bulk at the first charging state to 4.5 V. It can be noticed that Mn L-edge shifts slightly to a lower energy, revealing a lower Mn oxidation at the surface. The energy positions and fine structures of Fe L-edge and Ti L-edge spectra are basically identical from the surface to the bulk, as displayed in Figure S3 (Supporting Information), suggesting homogeneous valence states. An intensity decrease appears in the pre-edge (about 530 eV, indicated with a dotted line) of the O K-edge spectra, indicating that oxygen anions have been involved in the charging compensation process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0035_R_d142192342e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">15</a></sup></span> The charge compensation mechanism will be explained in a subsequent chapter.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202405620-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/65a2c0ec-f646-489c-bb9e-a46f821f4d78/anie202405620-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/65a2c0ec-f646-489c-bb9e-a46f821f4d78/anie202405620-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b429eb56-71ff-465b-b670-839e6051ff32/anie202405620-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/65a2c0ec-f646-489c-bb9e-a46f821f4d78/anie202405620-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202405620-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202405620" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>a, b) Charge and discharge curves and c) Cycling performances of MCNFT and NMO within a voltage range of 2.0–4.5 V at 10 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Inset shows the capacity retention over 15 cycles. d) Line-scan EELS spectra recorded from the surface to the bulk. e) Energy barriers and pathways for Na<sup>+</sup> migration in Na<sub>0.31</sub>[TM]O<sub>2</sub>. f) The lattice parameters with varying Na contents (x) in MCNFT and NMO from x=0.67 to 0.06.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The evolution of electronic structure and the variation of lattice parameters were collected from DFT calculations to explain the enhanced Na migration mechanism. The crystal structure of 0.31Na contained was used as an investigating model which is consistent with the 0.36Na extraction of the NMO comparison material. The Na migration energy barriers at <i>x</i>=0.31 in Na<sub>x</sub>[TM]O<sub>2</sub> (indicated Na<sub>x</sub>MCNFTO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>) were collected based on CINEB algorithm.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0036_R_d142192342e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16">16</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0002">2e</a>, the energy barrier of MCNFT is signally smaller than that of NMO. And the results of total-DOS displays that the MCNFT exhibits a higher electronic density of states and a narrower band gap in the vicinity of E<sub>F</sub>, implying a higher redox activity (Figure S4, Supporting Information). Moreover, the Crystal Orbital Hamilton Population (COHP) analysis was conducted to analyze the Mn−O covalency interactions presented in Figure S5 (Supporting Information). The average integrated COHP of MCNFT is higher than that of NMO, indicating a strengthened bonding interaction, which contributes to stabilize the oxygen oxidization during charging process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0039_R_d142192342e933" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> The charge density analysis on (100) plane in Figure S6 (Supporting Information) demonstrates a high Ti−O covalent interaction that is beneficial to the structural stability and Na migration.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0040_R_d142192342e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span> In addition, we compared the lattice parameters of Na<sub>x</sub>[TM]O<sub>2</sub> when <i>x</i>=0.67, 0.31, and 0.06 to evaluate the structural distortion during the first charging process, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0002">2f</a> and Table S3 (Supporting Information). The lower variations of these parameters indicate a stable crystal structure of MCNFT which are consistent with the results of calculations and cycling performance. Overall, this MCNFT designed from high entropy strategy demonstrates strong structural stability in a deeply desodiated state and has an extended cycling life.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202405620-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202405620-sec-0005-title"> Electrochemical Property</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical properties of the designed MCNFT samples were evaluated alongside normal NMO samples, as showing in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0003">3</a>. The typical charge–discharge profiles at 0.1 C of MCNFT and NMO in the voltage range of 2.0–4.3 V are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0003">3b</a>, respectively. The MCNFT sample exhibits a high capacity of 146 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> during the first charging process with a longer slope and plateau curve compared to the 83.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of the NMO sample. The noteworthy difference can be attributed to the conceivable cation redox activities of Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup>, Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0020_R_d142192342e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8c - Adv. Sci.">8c</a>, <a href="#anie202405620-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0043_R_d142192342e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Cent. Sci.">19</a></sup></span> It should be noted that the theoretical charging capacity of MCNFT is calculated to be 129.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> according to the stoichiometry of TM atoms, which is lower than that of the experimental results. The surplus capacity is attributed to anionic redox reactions, aligning with the findings in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0001">1f</a>, which will be further elaborated on in the sections addressing the charging compensation mechanism.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0034_R_d142192342e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#anie202405620-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0044_R_d142192342e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span> The experimental results reveal that there is only 0.11Na remaining in the lattice structure of MCNFT, significantly lower than the 0.35Na in NMO. The ICE of MCNFT is 97.2 %, superior to the 59.9 % observed in NMO. This suggests that more intrinsic Na ions in MCNFT can be activated and extracted during charging process. And the typical charging and discharging curves of the first three cycles are shown in Figure S7 (Supporting Information) which demonstrate that the MCNFT offer a higher and more reversible capacity than NMO. The results show a notable enhancement in the reversibility of the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion process, consistent with the findings in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0002">2b</a>.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202405620-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7674c1f8-4ed2-4201-8fe2-39ce3863a64f/anie202405620-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7674c1f8-4ed2-4201-8fe2-39ce3863a64f/anie202405620-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3235c756-dc72-409f-882a-873ed853f9bf/anie202405620-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7674c1f8-4ed2-4201-8fe2-39ce3863a64f/anie202405620-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202405620-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202405620" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Typical charge–discharge profiles of a) MCNFT and b) NMO between 2.0 and 4.3 V. The sodium content of MCNFT and NMO is approximately 0.11 and 0.36 respectively in the final charging state. c) Comparison of capacities at different cut-off voltages calculated by cyclic voltammetry tests. d) Energy densities based on the electrode materials. e) GITT curves and corresponding sodium-ion diffusion coefficients D<sub>Na+</sub>. f) Cycling performances of MCNFT and NMO at the current density of 2 C.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The operating voltage fluctuations for both MNCFT and NMO were tested by the dQ/dV profile, as illustrated in Figure S8 (Supporting Information). The highlighted area within the red frame indicates the presence of an additional couple of redox peaks in MCNFT. Furthermore, cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were conducted at different cut-off voltages (2.5 V, 3.0 V, 3.2 V, 3.5 V, 3.7 V, 4.0 V, 4.15 V, 4.3 V) to segmentally identify the capacity, as shown in Figure S9 (Supporting Information). In Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0003">3c</a>, a comparison of the area ratios enclosed by CV curves is presented. It is evident that the capacity of MCNFT above the voltage range of 3.5 V has doubled compared to NMO. Consequently, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0003">3d</a>, the ideal energy density of MCNFT reaches to 335 W h kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the total mass of cathode and anode materials. The result remarkably surpasses the 182 W h kg<sup>−1</sup> of NMO.</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was carried out to evaluate the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> migration. The variation trend of D<sub>Na+</sub> of the MCNFT is coherent and homogeneous, which is superior to that of NMO. The desodiation steric hindrance caused by the phase transition from P-type to O-type upon 3.6 V as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0003">3e</a> (grey shaded area) was eliminated in MCNFT, owing to the multi-elements synergistic effect.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0045_R_d142192342e1057" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span> Detailed measurement parameters can be found in Figure S10 (Supporting Information). Moreover, the specific capacity and cycle performance of MCNFT are more desirable than that of NMO, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0003">3f</a>. The capacity retention of the MCNFT is 97.1 % after 50 cycles at 2 C and the ICE is 100.7 % of the MCNFT, which is superior to 55.3 % of NMO. It is noticeable that MCNFT releases higher average discharging voltage of 3.05 V after enduring fifty cycles in Figure S11a (Supporting Information). The rate capability was evaluated at 0.1 C, 0.2 C, 0.5 C, 1 C, 2 C, 5 C, and 10 C. As shown in Figure S11b (Supporting Information), the MCNFT sample offers high specific capacity of 150.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 149.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 135.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 120.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 103.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 81.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and 62.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The long cycle life is described at a current density of 5 C, and the average capacity drop of MCNFT is 0.045 % per-cycle shown in Figure S11c (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>In order to further explore the application prospect of MCNFT, the full cells were assembled utilizing hard carbon (HC) materials as negative electrodes. The negative to positive capacity ratio (N/P) is 1 : 1.1. As shown in Figure S12 (Supporting Information), the MCNFT||HC full cells display an initial charging capacity of 140.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is obviously higher than that 85.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of NMO. What is more, the operating voltage of the MCNFT||HC full cell was 2.73 V after enduring fifty cycles compared to 2.29 V of the NMO||HC full cell. Similar results were obtained for the energy density calculations. The MCNFT cell system delivers an outstanding energy density of 356.6 W h kg<sup>−1</sup>, which is twice as high as the 175.8 W h kg<sup>−1</sup> of the NMO cell system. Therefore, the MCNFT sample exhibits significant improvements in operating voltage, specific capacity, and energy density.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202405620-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202405620-sec-0006-title"> Charging Compensation Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>The MCNFT sample was created by blending various transition metal elements combined with stimulated oxygen redox reactions for electrochemical capacity contributions. Previous reports have been confirmed that the oxygen redox reactions are not found in the NMO sample.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0009_R_d142192342e1100" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Rev.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202405620-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0048_R_d142192342e1100" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22">22</a></sup></span> Such oxygen redox could be a main factor contributing to the higher capacity of the designed MCNFT. O K-edge soft XAS measurement was carried out to investigate the charging compensation mechanism of anionic redox reaction at different charge states during electrochemical cycles in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0004">4a</a>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0035_R_d142192342e1106" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">15</a>, <a href="#anie202405620-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0051_R_d142192342e1106" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a></sup></span> These two peaks appeared in gray shaded region can be distributed to the electronic transition from O 1s orbit to the empty Mn 3d orbit mixed with O 2p orbitals.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0023_R_d142192342e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9b - Nat. Chem.">9b</a>, <a href="#anie202405620-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0052_R_d142192342e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Engl.">24</a></sup></span> And the integrated intensity calculated from 527 eV to 532 eV in this gray shaded region reveals the variation of the density of empty states, as exhibited in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0004">4b</a>. The relative integrated intensity consistently increases in the charging process and decreases in the discharging process. This variation can be undisputedly connected to the removal of electrons from oxygen and the formation of electron-holes on oxygen. Moreover, the XPS measurement was carried out to semiquantitative detect the oxygen species after etching 30 nm at the states of pristine, charging to 4.3 V and discharging to 2.0 V. In Figure S13 (Supporting Information), the peak (red trace) at 530.9 eV can be ascribed to the oxidation lattice oxygen (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0053_R_d142192342e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Mater.">25</a></sup></span> And the (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup> proportion of is 18.8 % according to the calculation of peak area ratio. Therefore, the oxygen redox reaction has been activated to contribute to the charging compensation.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202405620-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/60d578b7-bae6-49d4-9bb5-081882504de6/anie202405620-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/60d578b7-bae6-49d4-9bb5-081882504de6/anie202405620-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dbe244e8-82ae-4c8b-8f94-0e92720ac020/anie202405620-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/60d578b7-bae6-49d4-9bb5-081882504de6/anie202405620-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202405620-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202405620" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Charge compensation mechanism of MCNFT. a) O K-edge sXAS profiles at different states of charge. b) The variation of integrated intensities of the shadow ragion in (a). c) XANES spectra and d) corresponding EXAFS spectra at Mn K-edge. e) Peak fitting results of XPS spectra Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>, Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>, and Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> of MCNFT.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The charging compensation delivered from TM elements was measured via ex situ XAS and XPS. The X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra of MCNFT at Mn K-edge displays in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0004">4c</a> to illustrate the valence change of Mn, together with corresponding Fourier transformed extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0004">4d</a>. It should be noticed that the absorption edge of Mn K-edge shifts towards high energy direction, indicating that Mn has been oxidated to a higher valence state. This point can be confirmed by the shortened distance of Mn−O coordination in Figure 4e. Splitting and fitting results of peaks of XPS spectra can reflect the different valence states. In Figure S14 and S15a (Supporting Information), As for Mn, Ni, Fe, Cu elements, there are Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>, Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup> redox couple existing in the spectrum<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0020_R_d142192342e1189" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8c - Adv. Sci.">8c</a>, <a href="#anie202405620-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0054_R_d142192342e1189" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26">26</a></sup></span> During the charging process, along with Na<sup>+</sup> disembedding unremittingly, the proportion of high valence state ions increased. On the contrary, the valence states of all Mn, Ni, Fe, and Cu elements decrease during the discharging process. The corresponding refined peak area ratio are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0004">4e</a> and Figure S15b (Supporting Information). And the peaks of the Ti element barely change during the electrochemical cycles which could be indexed to Ti<sup>4+</sup> entirely.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0042_R_d142192342e1200" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18b - Adv. Mater.">18b</a>, <a href="#anie202405620-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0059_R_d142192342e1200" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nat. Commun.">27</a></sup></span> The doped Cu<sup>2+</sup> and Ni<sup>2+</sup> contributes to improve the activity of oxygen redox reaction and facilitates the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, while the doped elements of Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> help to suppress the phase transition and stabilize structure in deeply desodiated states. And the redox reaction of transition metal elements Mn, Ni, Fe, Cu combined with the activated O together offered the charging specific capacity.</p>
            
            <p>The pDOS calculations were carried out to better understand the charging compensation mechanism and the redox characteristics of elements based on the chemical formula of Na<sub>x</sub>MCNFTO<sub>2</sub> (x=0.67, 0.11). We made a comparison of density of states in the vicinity of E<sub>F</sub> between x=0.67 and x=0.11 in Figure S16 (Supporting Information). It can be summarized that Mn, Cu, Ni, Fe cooperatively donate electrons during the charging process and Ti exhibits electrochemical inertia in this voltage range. The reactivity of transition metal elements is in close alignment with consensus.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0020_R_d142192342e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8c - Adv. Sci.">8c</a>, <a href="#anie202405620-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0060_R_d142192342e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28">28</a></sup></span> It exhibits that oxygen can donate electron from initial state to the fully charged state based on the thermodynamical view. We believe that the redox reaction of oxygen is influenced by kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0025_R_d142192342e1226" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> In summary, the theoretical results align well with the experimental results, supporting the effectiveness of employing a high-entropy strategy with appropriate anionic redox activity to enhance stability at low sodium levels and the ICE.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202405620-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202405620-sec-0007-title"> Structure Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>To further clarify the improved electrochemical properties and understand the reaction mechanism, in situ XRD analysis was conducted on MCNFT during the initial two cycles. The patterns were collected and shown in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0005">5a</a> combining with the corresponding contour plot. The diffraction peaks shift along with the intercalation and deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>. During the charging process, the (002) and (004) peaks shift to a lower angle, which can be attributed to the expansion of c axis that generated from the increase of repulsion force between adjacent oxygen layers. Meanwhile, the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> also makes the (102) peak shift to a higher angle that can be ascribed to the decrease of bond length between adjacent in-plane TM atoms. During the following discharging process, there exists a completely reversible reaction and the corresponding diffraction peaks shift back to the pristine state. When charging to the high-voltage region over 4.1 V, there is an swelling between 16° and 17°, indicating that the intergrowth structure appears consisting of P-phase and O-phase.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0063_R_d142192342e1246" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a></sup></span> This typical phenomena is generally attributed to the oxygen redox reaction.<span><sup><a href="#anie202405620-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0024_R_d142192342e1249" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9c - Adv. Energy Mater.">9c</a>, <a href="#anie202405620-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202405620-bib-0064_R_d142192342e1249" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Lett.">30</a></sup></span> Moreover, the Rietveld refinement were carried out to measure the variation of lattice parameters accurately (Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0005">5b</a>). The variations exhibit 1.37 % for Δ a, 1.1 % for Δ b and 1.1 % for Δ c, indicating a stable structure enduring Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation. Overall, the MCNFT demonstrates a more stable crystal structure than NMO during electrochemical cycles owing to the high entropy strategy.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202405620-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/92ba2788-e202-41c7-b495-fb436fd6dc5c/anie202405620-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/92ba2788-e202-41c7-b495-fb436fd6dc5c/anie202405620-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/85bf03e2-ece5-409e-a4d1-e0a269f62062/anie202405620-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/92ba2788-e202-41c7-b495-fb436fd6dc5c/anie202405620-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202405620-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202405620" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Structural stability. a) In situ XRD patterns of MCNFT sample collected during the first two cycles at 0.2 C. b) Corresponding variation of lattice parameters a, c, and V of MCNFT and NMO. c) Schematic diagram of Na<sup>+</sup> migration.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The schematic graph in Figure <a href="#anie202405620-fig-0005">5c</a> illustrates that the MCNFT material achieves deeply desodiated stability through a high entropy strategy, whereas the deep extraction of the intrinsic Na<sup>+</sup> in NMO is not feasible. Therefore, the stable crystal structure and transition metal redox reactions, along with anionic redox reactions, effectively enhance energy density and ICE. The high entropy strategy has been theoretically and experimentally confirmed to enhance the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> and increase the stability of the cathode in high desodiated states.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202405620-sec-0008">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202405620-sec-0008-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, A new MCNFT is designed and synthesized based on the high-entropy strategy to enhance stability in deeply desodiated states, facilitate intrinsic Na<sup>+</sup> migration, and subsequently improve both the charging capacity and the ICE. This new MCNFT shows promising results, with 0.61Na extracted and demonstrating superior reversibility compared to NMO. Ex situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) studies reveal that the increased charging capacity from 92.8 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> to 158.1 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> when charging to 4.5 V, along with the rise in ICE from 57.7 % to 98.2 %, is attributed to synergistic cationic and anionic redox reactions. Additionally, the in situ XRD results indicate that the crystal structure is stabilized in the high desodiation state. Theoretical density functional theory (DFT) calculations are in accordance with these experimental findings. This research showcases the referential significance of utilizing high-energy density and cost-effective practical cathode materials in the development of sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202405620-sec-0009">

         </section>
